<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Liriodendron genome sheds light on angiosperm phylogeny and species&#8211;pair differentiation"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2018 5:1"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-12-17"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2018 The Author(s), under exclusive licence to Springer Nature Limited"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="The genus Liriodendron belongs to the family Magnoliaceae, which resides within the magnoliids, an early diverging lineage of the Mesangiospermae. However, the phylogenetic relationship of magnoliids with eudicots and monocots has not been conclusively resolved and thus remains to be determined1&#8211;6. Liriodendron is a relict lineage from the Tertiary with two distinct species&#8212;one East Asian (L. chinense (Hemsley) Sargent) and one eastern North American (L. tulipifera Linn)&#8212;identified as a vicariad species pair. However, the genetic divergence and evolutionary trajectories of these species remain to be elucidated at the whole-genome level7. Here, we report the first de novo genome assembly of a plant in the Magnoliaceae, L. chinense. Phylogenetic analyses suggest that magnoliids are sister to the clade consisting of eudicots and monocots, with rapid diversification occurring in the common ancestor of these three lineages. Analyses of population genetic structure indicate that L. chinense has diverged into two lineages&#8212;the eastern and western groups&#8212;in China. While L. tulipifera in North America is genetically positioned between the two L. chinense groups, it is closer to the eastern group. This result is consistent with phenotypic observations that suggest that the eastern and western groups of China may have diverged long ago, possibly before the intercontinental differentiation between L. chinense and L. tulipifera. Genetic diversity analyses show that L. chinense has tenfold higher genetic diversity than L. tulipifera, suggesting that the complicated regions comprising east&#8211;west-orientated mountains and the Yangtze river basin (especially near 30&#176;&#8201;N latitude) in East Asia offered more successful refugia than the south&#8211;north-orientated mountain valleys in eastern North America during the Quaternary glacial period. A high-quality reference genome for the plant Liriodendron chinense places magnoliids as sister to the clade consisting of eudicots and monocots. Population genomic analyses unravel the evolution of two distinct species in the genus Liriodendron."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>The genus <i>Liriodendron</i> belongs to the family Magnoliaceae, which resides within the magnoliids, an early diverging lineage of the Mesangiospermae. However, the phylogenetic relationship of magnoliids with eudicots and monocots has not been conclusively resolved and thus remains to be determined<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Soltis, P. S. &amp; Soltis, D. E. The origin and diversification of angiosperms. Am. J. Bot. 91, 1614–1626 (2004)." href="#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d4932e838">1</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Qui, Y. L. et al. Phylogenetic analyses of basal angiosperms based on nine plastid, mitochondrial, and nuclear genes. Int. J. Plant Sci. 166, 815–842 (2005)." href="#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4932e838_1">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Moore, M. J. &amp; Soltis, D. E. Phylogenetic analysis of the plastid inverted repeat for 244 species: insights into deeper-level angiosperm relationships from a long, slowly evolving sequence region. Int. J. Plant Sci. 172, 541–558 (2011)." href="#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d4932e838_2">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Zhang, N., Zeng, L., Shan, H. &amp; Ma, H. Highly conserved low-copy nuclear genes as effective markers for phylogenetic analyses in angiosperms. New Phytol. 195, 923–937 (2012)." href="#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d4932e838_3">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4932e838_4">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4932e841">6</a></sup>. <i>Liriodendron</i> is a relict lineage from the Tertiary with two distinct species—one East Asian (<i>L. chinense</i> (Hemsley) Sargent) and one eastern North American (<i>L. tulipifera</i> Linn)—identified as a vicariad species pair. However, the genetic divergence and evolutionary trajectories of these species remain to be elucidated at the whole-genome level<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Parks, C. R. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Molecular divergence between Asian and North American species of Liriodendron (Magnoliaceae) with implications for interpretation of fossil floras. Am. J. Bot. 77, 1243–1256 (1990)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4932e855">7</a></sup>. Here, we report the first de novo genome assembly of a plant in the Magnoliaceae, <i>L. chinense</i>. Phylogenetic analyses suggest that magnoliids are sister to the clade consisting of eudicots and monocots, with rapid diversification occurring in the common ancestor of these three lineages. Analyses of population genetic structure indicate that <i>L. chinense</i> has diverged into two lineages—the eastern and western groups—in China. While <i>L. tulipifera</i> in North America is genetically positioned between the two <i>L. chinense</i> groups, it is closer to the eastern group. This result is consistent with phenotypic observations that suggest that the eastern and western groups of China may have diverged long ago, possibly before the intercontinental differentiation between <i>L. chinense</i> and <i>L. tulipifera</i>. Genetic diversity analyses show that <i>L. chinense</i> has tenfold higher genetic diversity than <i>L. tulipifera</i>, suggesting that the complicated regions comprising east–west-orientated mountains and the Yangtze river basin (especially near 30° N latitude) in East Asia offered more successful refugia than the south–north-orientated mountain valleys in eastern North America during the Quaternary glacial period.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>The Magnoliaceae, a family in the order Magnoliales, is an early diverging lineage of the Mesangiospermae (core angiosperms)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Azuma, H., Garcia-Franco, J. G., Rico-Gray, V. &amp; Thien, L. B. Molecular phylogeny of the Magnoliaceae: the biogeography of tropical and temperate disjunctions. Am. J. Bot. 88, 2275–2285 (2001)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d4932e922">8</a></sup>, and thus, it possesses a crucial phylogenetic position for better understanding the evolution of the extant flowering plants. However, the relationships among magnoliids, eudicots, and monocots have not been conclusively resolved despite previous valuable attempts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Qui, Y. L. et al. Phylogenetic analyses of basal angiosperms based on nine plastid, mitochondrial, and nuclear genes. Int. J. Plant Sci. 166, 815–842 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d4932e926">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d4932e929">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4932e932">6</a></sup>. The <i>Liriodendron</i> genus, which belongs to the subfamily Liriodendroideae of the Magnoliaceae, consisted of several species distributed throughout the Northern Hemisphere until the Late Tertiary, but now comprises only of a pair of sister species with a classic intercontinental disjunction distribution: one in East Asia (<i>L. chinense</i>) and the other in eastern North America (<i>L. tulipifera</i>). These two Tertiary relict <i>Liriodendron</i> species have been suggested to have diverged during the middle to late Miocene<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Parks, C. R. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Molecular divergence between Asian and North American species of Liriodendron (Magnoliaceae) with implications for interpretation of fossil floras. Am. J. Bot. 77, 1243–1256 (1990)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4932e949">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Nie, Z. L. et al. Phylogenetic and biogeographic complexity of Magnoliaceae in the Northern Hemisphere inferred from three nuclear data sets. Mol. Phylogenet. Evol. 48, 1027–1040 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d4932e952">9</a></sup>, a reflection of range restrictions resulting from extinctions in the late Cenozoic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Parks, C. R., Miller, N. G., Wendel, J. F. &amp; McDougal, K. M. Genetic divergence within the genus Liriodendron (Magnoliaceae). Ann. MO Bot. Gard. 70, 658–666 (1983)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d4932e956">10</a></sup>. Moreover, this pair of species is a perfect verification of the second prediction of the geographic speciation theory, which was proposed to explain the origin of species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Darwin, C. R. On the Origin of Species by Means of Natural Selection or the Preservation of Favoured Races in the Struggle for Life (John Murray, London, 1859)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d4932e960">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Coyne, J. A. Why Evolution Is True (Univ. Press, Oxford, 2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d4932e963">12</a></sup>.</p><p>Here, we combined three different sequencing technologies (that is, short-read sequencing, long-read sequencing and optical mapping) to de novo assemble the <i>L. chinense</i> genome. First, we achieved ~327.11 gigabases (Gb) of clean Illumina paired-end reads (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">1</a>), ~147.89 Gb of corrected PacBio long reads (length longer than 2 kilobases (kb); Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">2</a>) and ~315.41 Gb of Bionano genome map data (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">3</a>). We estimated the genome size to be 1.75 Gb based on Illumina data (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">4</a>), which was consistent with the estimation of ~1.8 Gb using flow cytometry (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). Then, we assembled the genome of <i>Liriodendron</i> into 4,624 contigs with an N50 length of 1.43 megabases (Mb) using Falcon (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">5</a>). Furthermore, this assembly of long reads was integrated with a Bionano optical map to create a hybrid assembly consisting of 3,711 scaffolds totalling 1.74 Gb with an N50 length of 3.53 Mb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">5</a>). Finally, we anchored 529 scaffolds totalling ~1.37 Gb to a genetic map with 19 linkage groups, using a total of 1,576 microsatellite markers (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">2</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">6</a>). A high-confidence set of 35,269 gene models was constructed using the genome annotation pipeline MAKER (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">3</a>), with 83.59% of genes being assigned putative functional annotations (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">7</a>). To assess the quality of the assembly, we compared ten bacterial artificial chromosomes (BACs), in which potential repeat regions were masked (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), with assembled scaffolds, resulting in an average coverage of 99.75% (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">4</a>). Of all 66,934 unigenes (&gt;200 base pairs (bp)) assembled de novo by RNA sequencing (RNA-Seq), more than 90% had a length coverage of greater than 90% within a single scaffold (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">8</a>). In addition, 1,300 (90.28%) genes of the BUSCO plant set were covered by the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>The genome size of <i>L. chinense</i> is larger than those of most sequenced angiosperms (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">5</a>). We further investigated two pertinent aspects of genome evolution—whole-genome duplication (WGD) events and transposable element bursts—both of which have had profound effects on plant genome evolution<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wendel, J. F., Jackson, S. A., Meyers, B. C. &amp; Wing, R. A. Evolution of plant genome architecture. Genome Biol. 17, 37 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1039">13</a></sup>. The fraction of synonymous substitutions per synonymous site (<i>K</i><sub>s</sub>) distributions of paralogues in the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome and transcriptome clearly illustrate the occurrence of a single WGD event experienced by <i>Liriodendron</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig1">1a,b</a>). It has been firmly established that whole-genome triplication (mechanistically originating as two successive WGDs) occurred in the grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Jaillon, O. et al. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463–467 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1057">14</a></sup>, and there is no evidence for lineage-specific polyploidy events in <i>Amborella</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Amborella Genome, P. The Amborella genome and the evolution of flowering plants. Science 342, 1241089 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1063">15</a></sup>. By performing a comparative genomic analysis of <i>Vitis</i> with <i>Amborella</i> and <i>Liriodendron</i>, we identified 3:1 and 3:2 syntenic depth ratios in the <i>Vitis</i>–<i>Amborella</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">6</a>) and <i>Vitis</i><i>–Liriodendron</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig1">1c</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">7</a>) comparisons, respectively. Furthermore, we mapped the complete repertoire of 1–2–3 orthologous regions in the <i>Amborella–Liriodendron</i>–<i>Vitis</i> genome comparison (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig1">1d,e</a>). Thus, from these data, we conclude that a single <i>Liriodendron</i> lineage-specific WGD event occurred, consistent with the results of the fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion position analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">8</a>). We speculated that the <i>Liriodendron</i> WGD event occurred approximately 116 million years ago (Ma) with a synonymous substitution rate of 3.02 × 10<sup>−9</sup> synonymous substitutions yr<sup>−</sup><sup>1</sup> (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738–749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1123">16</a></sup>). Considering the possibly overestimated synonymous substitution rate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Cui, L. et al. Widespread genome duplications throughout the history of flowering plants. Genome Res. 16, 738–749 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1127">16</a></sup> and the divergence time of 113–128 Ma between the families Magnoliaceae and Lauraceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Kumar, S., Stecher, G., Suleski, M. &amp; Hedges, S. B. TimeTree: a resource for timelines, timetrees, and divergence times. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 1812–1819 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1131">17</a></sup>, the WGD detected in the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome probably predated the separation of these two families.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Liriodendron lineage-specific WGD."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: <i>Liriodendron</i> lineage-specific WGD.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="629" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distributions for the whole paranome identified from the whole genome of <i>Liriodendron</i> (green), grape (blue) and <i>Amborella</i> (orange). WGT, whole-genome triplication. <b>b</b>, <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> distribution for the whole paranome identified from the whole transcriptome of <i>L. chinense</i>. <b>c</b>, Comparison of <i>Liriodendron</i> and grape genomes. Dot plots of orthologues show a 2–3 chromosomal relationship between the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome and grape genome. <b>d</b>, Macrosynteny patterns show that a typical ancestral region in the basal angiosperm <i>Amborella</i> can be tracked to up to two regions in <i>Liriodendron</i> and to up to three regions in the grape. Grey wedges in the background highlight major syntenic blocks spanning more than 30 genes between the genomes (highlighted by one syntenic set shown in colour). <b>e</b>, Microcollinearity patterns between genomic regions from <i>Amborella</i>, <i>Liriodendron</i> and the grape. Rectangles represent predicted gene models, with purple and brown showing relative gene orientations. Grey wedges connect matching gene pairs, with two sets highlighted in red.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Transposable elements account for 61.64% of the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">11</a>). Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons are the most abundant transposable element, representing 56.25% of the assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">11</a>). Among the LTR retrotransposons, <i>Gypsy</i> elements are much more abundant than <i>Copia</i> elements (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">9</a>). In addition, transposable elements are unevenly distributed across the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome and tend to accumulate in intergenic regions rather than genic regions and regions adjoining genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">10</a>), probably as a result of natural selection due to the potential detrimental effects of transposable elements on gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Hollister, J. D. &amp; Gaut, B. S. Epigenetic silencing of transposable elements: a trade-off between reduced transposition and deleterious effects on neighboring gene expression. Genome Res. 19, 1419–1428 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1235">18</a></sup>. With respect to the genic regions, transposable elements have an unequal distribution between exons and introns, and there is an obvious bias towards transposable element accumulation in introns compared with exons (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">11</a>), consistent with the natural selection hypothesis, although introns may play an important role in gene expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Rose, A. B. &amp; Beliakoff, J. A. Intron-mediated enhancement of gene expression independent of unique intron sequences and splicing. Plant Physiol. 122, 535–542 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1243">19</a></sup>. Furthermore, long interspersed nuclear element-1 has an abnormally high rate of accumulation in genic regions, in contrast with the pattern shown by other transposable elements (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">12</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">13</a>). Moreover, we analysed the divergence time distribution for all LTRs in the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome and found a <i>K</i><sub>s</sub> peak at 0.05 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">13</a>). We assumed an intergenic nucleotide substitution rate of 1.51 × 10<sup>−9</sup> that was roughly twice as low as that within the genic regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), resulting in an insertion time of ~16 Ma. Overall, these results show that an ancient WGD event that occurred approximately 116 Ma, followed by a more recent burst of transposable element insertion that occurred approximately 16 Ma, have both contributed to the expansion of the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome.</p><p>Some features of the <i>Liriodendron</i> phenotype are typical of both monocots and eudicots (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig2">2a</a>), which is consistent with the obscure phylogenetic relationships among magnoliids, monocots and eudicots. To investigate which of the three previously proposed tree topologies is most likely to be true (that is: (1) ((monocots, (eudicots, magnoliids)), basal angiosperm); (2) ((eudicots, (monocots, magnoliids)), basal angiosperm); or (3) ((magnoliids, (monocots, eudicots)), basal angiosperm) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">14</a>)), we selected an additional six eudicots, six monocots, three magnoliids and one basal angiosperm, with one gymnosperm being the outgroup (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">14</a>), to construct individual orthogroups. In this way, we could use as many gene families as possible to depict a broad picture of the phylogeny. After careful evaluation and selection (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), we finally obtained 502 low-copy orthogroups, with 172 orthogroups (34.26%) supporting topology I, 155 orthogroups (30.88%) supporting topology II and the final 175 orthogroups (34.86%) supporting topology III (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig2">2b</a>), with no statistically significant difference among the three topologies (<i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> = 1.3904; <i>P</i> = 0.4990). Based on these 502 low-copy orthogroups, quantification of differences in gene-wise log-likelihood scores (ΔGLS) among these three alternative topologies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Shen, X. X., Hittinger, C. T. &amp; Rokas, A. Contentious relationships in phylogenomic studies can be driven by a handful of genes. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 126 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1302">20</a></sup> showed an equal distribution of phylogenetic signals for each topology at the gene level (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">16</a>). Further excluding orthogroups whose ΔGLS values were outliers (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>), we obtained 481 low-copy orthogroups, with a lack of statistical significance among the orthogroups supporting each of the three alternative topologies (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig2">2b</a>; <i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.2162; <i>P</i> = 0.8975). These results explain why all three possible topologies have been observed in previous studies using different datasets (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">14</a>) and suggest that rapid diversification occurred in the common ancestor of magnoliids, eudicots and monocots, which might be responsible for the phylogenetic incongruence in previous studies.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Phylogenetic relationships among magnoliids, eudicots and monocots."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Phylogenetic relationships among magnoliids, eudicots and monocots.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="576" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, <i>Liriodendron</i> shows typical features of monocots in its reproductive organs (flower parts in multiples of three and monosulcate pollen grains) and of eudicots in its vegetative organs (two cotyledons, a taproot system, a eudicot-like stem cross-section and netted venation). These experiments were repeated independently at least ten times with similar results. Scale bar, 200 µm. <b>b</b>, Three topologies that coincided with three alternative phylogenetic hypotheses are plotted, and the results of a chi-squared test of the orthogroup numbers supporting each topology are shown below, revealing no statistically significant difference in topology preference. <b>c</b>, The eudicot- and monocot-specific gene families present in <i>Liriodendron</i> are statistically similar to those present in <i>Amborella</i>, whereas <i>Spirodela polyrhiza</i> has a bias towards monocot-specific gene families, and <i>Macleaya cordata</i> has a bias towards eudicot-specific gene families when compared with <i>Amborella</i>. <b>d</b>, Dated phylogeny for 11 plant species with <i>Picea abies</i> as an outgroup. A time scale is shown at the bottom, and red points in some nodes indicate fossil calibration points.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To further confirm the <i>Liriodendron</i> phylogeny, a coalescent-based species tree was constructed using the 502-orthogroup dataset, and this tree supported topology III with low bootstrap support (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">17a</a>). Additionally, we performed coalescent-based species tree construction based on the 481-orthogroup dataset, yielding a topology identical to topology III with a bootstrap value increasing from 50 to 54% (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">17b</a>). Furthermore, we performed a phylogenetic analysis on the basis of a concatenated sequence alignment of 78 chloroplast genes, yielding a topology consistent with topology III with strong bootstrap support (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">18</a>). To continue our investigation, we identified both eudicot- and monocot-specific gene families present in the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome based on the PLAZA 3.0 Monocots database (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">19</a>). The gene families from either clade were not significantly over-represented in <i>Liriodendron</i> compared with <i>Amborella</i> (<i>χ</i><sup>2</sup> = 0.1166; <i>P</i> = 0.7328), whereas a monocot plant and a eudicot plant both showed significant biases towards their respective gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig2">2c</a>). Overall, considering our results, including the mosaic phenotypic characterization, individual and multiple gene tree reconstructions, and lineage-specific gene family identification, we suggest a topology in which eudicots and monocots form a clade that is sister to magnoliids, represented by <i>Liriodendron</i>, with the basal angiosperm <i>Amborella</i> being the next group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig2">2d</a>); that is, magnoliids arose before the divergence of eudicots and monocots. Thus, the phylogenetic analysis incorporating the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome provides additional insights into the systematic position and evolution of magnoliids.</p><p>At present, the <i>Liriodendron</i> genus contains only two species in regions with a humid subtropical climate, and has partially expanded to the southern margin of the warm temperate climate zone of the Northern Hemisphere<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Little, E. L. Atlas of United States Trees. Volume 1: Conifers and Important Hardwoods (US Department of Agriculture, 1971)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1426">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Hao, R. M. Geographical distribution of Liriodendron chinense in China and its significance. J. Plant Resour. Environ. (China) 4, 1–6 (1995)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1429">22</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">20</a>). However, a number of extinct <i>Liriodendron</i> species were once widely distributed in relatively high-latitude regions of the Northern Hemisphere before a general cooling of the climate occurred during in the Late Tertiary<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Milne, R. I. Northern Hemisphere plant disjunctions: a window on tertiary land bridges and climate change? Ann. Bot. 98, 465–472 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1443">23</a></sup>, based on fossil records of seeds and leaves (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">21</a>). To explore the historical demographic fluctuations and present-day genetic diversity within these two <i>Liriodendron</i> species, we resequenced 20 <i>Liriodendron</i> accessions, including 14 <i>L. chinense</i> individuals and six <i>L. tulipifera</i> individuals (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3a</a>, Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">22</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">15</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Geographic distribution and population diversity of Liriodendron accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Geographic distribution and population diversity of <i>Liriodendron</i> accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="645" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Geographic distribution of <i>Liriodendron</i> accessions. Brown triangles represent the fossil distribution of <i>Liriodendron</i> plants in high-latitude regions of the Northern Hemisphere. Fringe patterns show two principal refugia where Tertiary relict floras occurred: southern East Asia and eastern North America. The natural distributions of <i>L. chinense</i> and <i>L. tulipifera</i> are plotted, with coloured dots representing individual <i>Liriodendron</i> accessions. <b>b</b>, Neighbour-joining tree of all accessions constructed from whole-genome SNPs. Accessions coming from the same geographic areas are grouped together and coloured corresponding to the colours used in <b>a</b>. LY, Liu Yang; SZ, Sang Zhi; EX, E Xi; ST, Song Tao; LP, Li Ping; ML, Meng La; XY, Xu Yong; SN, Sui Ning; DBS, DaBie Shan; HS, Huang Shan; LS1, Lushan_1; SY, Song Yang; LS2, Lushan_2; WYS, WuYi Shan; ON, Ontario; LA, Louisiana; GA, Georgia; TN, Tennessee; NC, North Carolina; MO, Missouri. <b>c</b>, Principal component analysis plots of the first two components for all 20 accessions, with dots coloured corresponding to their provenances. <b>d</b>, Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) and population divergence (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) across the three groups. The value in each circle represents a measure of nucleotide diversity for this group, and the value on each line indicates the population divergence between the two groups.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>On the basis of phylogenetic analysis of a whole-genome single nucleotide polymorphism (SNP) analysis, we found that these <i>Liriodendron</i> accessions formed three distinct phylogenetic groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3b</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">23</a>). This was further supported by a principal component analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3c</a>) and structure analysis (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">24</a>). All <i>L. chinense</i> individuals from western China (CW) clustered together, and the rest of the <i>L. chinense</i>, collected from eastern China (CE), clustered into the second group. The third group comprised all <i>L. tulipifera</i> individuals collected from North America (NA). It is evident that the NA group is phylogenetically positioned between the two <i>L. chinense</i> groups and more closely related to the CE group, suggesting that the earliest divergence occurred between the populations in eastern China and those in western China, followed by differentiation between the eastern Chinese populations and North American populations. This pattern is supported by the phenotypic analysis, which shows that all three groups share one leaf morphological feature, while the CE and NA groups have their own unique leaf morphological feature (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">25</a>). Fossil records indicate that similar leaf morphological features to those in the western and eastern China groups had already emerged in two extinct <i>Liriodendron</i> species<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Baghai, N. L. Liriodendron (Magnoliaceae) from the Miocene Clarkia flora of Idaho. Am. J. Bot. 75, 451–464 (1988)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1564">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Bell, W. A. Upper Cretaceous floras of the Dunvegan, Bad Heart, and Milk River Formations of Western Canada. Geol. Surv. Canada Bull. 94, 1–76 (1962)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1567">25</a></sup>, again suggesting that these two <i>L. chinense</i> groups may have diverged a very long time ago, possibly preceding the intercontinental differentiation between <i>L. chinense</i> and <i>L. tulipifera</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">25</a>).</p><p>Nucleotide diversity (<i>π</i>) analysis shows that the CW group has the highest genetic diversity, followed by the CE group, and that the genetic diversity of the NA group is tenfold lower than that of the CW group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3d</a>). An analysis of demographic history using the pairwise sequentially Markovian coalescent (PSMC) model<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Inference of human population history from individual whole-genome sequences. Nature 475, 493–496 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1592">26</a></sup> shows that the two groups from China both had population size peaks at approximately 0.4 Ma and declined afterwards, whereas the NA group population size peak occurred much earlier and continuously declined since approximately 2.3 Ma (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig4">4</a>), indicating that the populations in eastern China and those in western China underwent a similar demographic history different from that in North American populations. We also calculated genetic differentiation statistics (fixation index; <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub>) among the three <i>Liriodendron</i> groups, indicating that the genetic differentiation (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> = 0.2055) between the NA group and the CW group was slightly lower than that (<i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> = 0.2707) between the NA group and the CE group (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3d</a>). In addition, we also found that the CW group had the highest level of individual differences compared with the other two geographical groups (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">26</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Historical fluctuations in effective population size."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Historical fluctuations in effective population size.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-018-0323-6/MediaObjects/41477_2018_323_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1409" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p><b>a</b>–<b>c</b>, Plots of PSMC results for 20 individuals (7 from western China (<b>a</b>); 7 from eastern China (<b>b</b>); and 6 from North America (<b>c</b>)), as indicated in each legend. The grey lines represent the mass accumulation rate (MAR) of the Chinese Loess Plateau in <b>a</b> and <b>b</b>, and the atmospheric surface air temperature relative to the present in <b>c</b>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The natural distribution areas of these two <i>Liriodendron</i> species on their respective continents are highly consistent with the two principal areas where Tertiary relict floras occur<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Milne, R. I. Northern Hemisphere plant disjunctions: a window on tertiary land bridges and climate change? Ann. Bot. 98, 465–472 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1661">23</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3a</a>). Although <i>Liriodendron</i> species were once distributed over the high-latitude regions of Europe (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3a</a>), the east–west-orientated mountains are thought to have blocked their southward migration during global cooling in the Late Tertiary and subsequent Quaternary glaciations<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tiffney, B. H. The Eocene North Atlantic land bridge: its importance in Tertiary and modern phytogeography of the Northern Hemisphere. J. Arnold Arbor. 66, 243–273 (1985)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1675">27</a></sup>, finally leading to the extinction of <i>Liriodendron</i> in Europe<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Parks, C. R. &amp; Wendel, J. F. Molecular divergence between Asian and North American species of Liriodendron (Magnoliaceae) with implications for interpretation of fossil floras. Am. J. Bot. 77, 1243–1256 (1990)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1682">7</a></sup>. With respect to the <i>Liriodendron</i> that survived in East Asia and eastern North America, the higher genetic diversity of <i>L. chinense</i> compared with <i>L. tulipifera</i> is consistent with the greater number of suitable refugia in East Asia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Wen, J. Evolution of eastern Asian and eastern North American disjunct distributions in flowering plants. Annu. Rev. Ecol. Syst. 30, 421–455 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1696">28</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Qian, H. &amp; Ricklefs, R. E. Large-scale processes and the Asian bias in species diversity of temperate plants. Nature 407, 180–182 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1699">29</a></sup>. In this study, we observed a sustained population decrease during the whole Quaternary glaciation in all <i>L. tulipifera</i> accessions and a population recovery approximately 0.3–0.4 Ma in all <i>L. chinense</i> accessions (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig4">4</a>), which may have contributed considerably to the severe loss of genetic diversity in <i>L. tulipifera</i> and the relatively high retention of genetic diversity in <i>L. chinense</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#Fig3">3d</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#MOESM1">27</a>), respectively. The population recovery observed in all <i>L. chinense</i> accessions occurred in the interglacial stage between the Guxiang Glaciation (0.3–0.13 Ma) and Naynayxungla Glaciation (0.72–0.5 Ma)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Sun, Y. B. &amp; An, Z. S. Late Pliocene–Pleistocene changes in mass accumulation rates of eolian deposits on the central Chinese Loess Plateau. J. Geophys. Res. Atmos. 110, D23101 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1728">30</a></sup>. Considering that the Naynayxungla Glaciation was the most extensive glaciation, including large ice caps and massive valley glaciers, and the following Guxiang Glaciation was characterized by valley glaciers only<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Sun, Y. B. &amp; An, Z. S. Late Pliocene–Pleistocene changes in mass accumulation rates of eolian deposits on the central Chinese Loess Plateau. J. Geophys. Res. Atmos. 110, D23101 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1732">30</a></sup>, we speculate that the temperature recovery and deglaciation during this interglacial stage provided a foundation for <i>L. chinense</i> population recovery within East Asian refugia. Consequently, in addition to the higher habitat diversity within East Asian refugia<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Qian, H. &amp; Ricklefs, R. E. Large-scale processes and the Asian bias in species diversity of temperate plants. Nature 407, 180–182 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1740">29</a></sup>, a suitable living environment during the interglacial stage between the Naynayxungla and Guxiang glaciations may have contributed to the retention, restoration and augmentation of <i>L. chinense</i> genetic diversity.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Plant materials and sequencing</h3><p>For genome sequencing, we collected fresh leaves from an adult plant of <i>L. chinense</i> grown in Lushan, which is located in the Jiangxi province of China. For Illumina sequencing, four series of paired-end sequencing libraries with insert sizes of 170, 250, 500 and 800 bp were constructed and subsequently sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform, ultimately resulting in 327.11 Gb clean reads. For PacBio single-molecule real-time sequencing, sequencing libraries with 20-kb DNA inserts were constructed and subsequently sequenced on the Pacific Biosciences RSII instrument, ultimately resulting in a total of 150.18 Gb subread with an N50 length of 15.96 kb for the genome assembly. In addition, purified DNA was labelled at Nt.BspQI sites using the IrysPrep kit, and a 315.41 Gb optical map of the sample was produced from the BioNano Irys system. In addition, abundances of 17-nucleotide k-mers from 170- and 250-bp Illumina sequencing libraries were used to estimate the genome size.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">De novo assembly</h3><p>The <i>Liriodendron</i> genome was de novo assembled using FALCON (<a href="https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON">https://github.com/PacificBiosciences/FALCON</a>) based on PacBio long reads (only reads longer than 10 kb were used in the assembly). Errors in the PacBio reads were corrected within the FALCON pipeline. Contigs was first polished based on raw PacBio data and finally corrected using Illumina short reads with Pilon<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Walker, B. J. et al. Pilon: an integrated tool for comprehensive microbial variant detection and genome assembly improvement. PLoS ONE 9, e112963 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1777">31</a></sup>. A hybrid assembly was created based on contigs and optical maps using the Bionano Solve Pipeline (<a href="https://bionanogenomics.com/support-page/bionano-access/">https://bionanogenomics.com/support-page/bionano-access/</a>). Then, the corrected PacBio long reads were used for superscaffold gap filling using PBJelly<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="English, A. C. et al. Mind the gap: upgrading genomes with Pacific Biosciences RS long-read sequencing technology. PLoS ONE 7, e47768 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1785">32</a></sup>. We constructed a reference genetic map of <i>L. chinense</i> based on an F<sub>1</sub> population of 150 plants from a cross between <i>L. chinense</i> and <i>L. tulipifera</i> using JoinMap 4.0 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Van Ooijen, J. W. JoinMap 4: Software for the Calculation of Genetic Linkage Maps in Experimental Populations (Kyazma B.V., Wageningen, 2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1801">33</a></sup>). Markers with inconsistent placement were manually screened and the collinearity of common markers was inspected using MapChart 2.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Van Ooijen, J. W. JoinMap 4: Software for the Calculation of Genetic Linkage Maps in Experimental Populations (Kyazma B.V., Wageningen, 2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1805">33</a></sup>). Markers in common were used as anchor points. Possible chimeric scaffolds were identified as those containing sequences of markers mapped to different locations in the same linkage group or different linkage groups, and these scaffolds were manually inspected. This process generated 19 <i>Liriodendron</i> pseudomolecules.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Genome assessment</h3><p>We assessed the coverage of the genome assembly by mapping 89 BACs back to assembly with 97% of these BAC sequences covered without any obvious misassemblies. A comparison of 9 randomly chosen BACs sequenced by 454 sequencing technology indicated a low error rate. In addition, we used the BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1821">34</a></sup> database to assess the genome assembly. We also validated the assembled genome using 66,934 unigenes (length ≥ 200 bp) from RNA-Seq.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Repeat annotation</h3><p>We identified tandem repeats and transposable elements separately. Tandem repeats were predicted using Tandem Repeats Finder 4.04 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573–580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1833">35</a></sup>). For transposable element identification, we performed a combination of similarity-based and de novo approaches. First, we used RepeatMasker with the Repbase 16.10 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Jurka, J. et al. Repbase Update, a database of eukaryotic repetitive elements. Cytogenet. Genome Res. 110, 462–467 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1837">36</a></sup>) database of known repeat sequences to search for transposable elements in the genome, and we additionally used RepeatProteinMask, implemented in RepeatMasker, to identify transposable elements by aligning the genome sequence to the transposable element protein database. Then, to apply our de novo approach, we constructed a repeat library generated by RepeatModeler<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21, i351–i358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1841">37</a></sup> with default parameters and ran RepeatMasker on the genome sequences, using the RepeatModeler consensus sequence as a library. Finally, all the repeat sequences identified by the different methods were combined into the final repeat annotation.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Gene prediction</h3><p>Gene model prediction was conducted by the MAKER pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Cantarel, B. L. et al. Maker: an easy-to-use annotation pipeline designed for emerging model organism genomes. Genome Res. 18, 188–196 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1854">38</a></sup>, integrating ab initio prediction with de novo assembled transcripts from short-read messenger RNA sequencing, isoform-sequencing full-length transcripts, and protein homology data. A high-confidence gene model was constructed by further removing transposons and low-confidence predictions. Gene functional annotation was performed using the Swiss-Prot and TrEMBL databases<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bairoch, A. &amp; Apweiler, R. The Swiss-Prot protein sequence database and its supplement trembl in 2000. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 45–48 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1858">39</a></sup>, while motifs and domains were annotated using InterProScan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Zdobnov, E. M. &amp; Apweiler, R. InterProScan—an integration platform for the signature-recognition methods in InterPro. Bioinformatics 17, 847–848 (2001)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1862">40</a></sup> by searching against publicly available protein databases. Descriptions of gene products (that is, Gene Ontology terms) were retrieved from the corresponding InterPro entries. We also mapped the <i>Liriodendron</i> reference genes to KEGG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Kanehisa, M. &amp; Goto, S. KEGG: kyoto encyclopedia of genes and genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 28, 27–30 (2000)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1869">41</a></sup> pathway maps.</p><p>Transfer RNA genes were predicted based on tRNAscan-SE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Lowe, T. M. &amp; Eddy, S. R. tRNAscan-SE: a program for improved detection of transfer RNA genes in genomic sequence. Nucleic Acids Res. 25, 955–964 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1876">42</a></sup>. Ribosomal RNA fragments were identified by aligning plant ribosomal RNA sequences<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Garcia, S., Galvez, F., Gras, A., Kovarik, A. &amp; Garnatje, T. Plant rDNA database: update and new features. Database 2014, 1–7 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1880">43</a></sup> to the <i>Liriodendron</i> genome by BLASTN<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Altschul, S. F., Gish, W., Miller, W., Myers, E. W. &amp; Lipman, D. J. Basic local alignment search tool. J. Mol. Biol. 215, 403–410 (1990)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1887">44</a></sup>. micro RNA and small nuclear RNA genes were detected by INFERNAL<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Nawrocki, E. P., Kolbe, D. L. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Infernal 1.0: inference of RNA alignments. Bioinformatics 25, 1335–1337 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1891">45</a></sup> software against the Rfam database<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Griffithsjones, S. et al. Rfam: annotating non-coding RNAs in complete genomes. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 121–124 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1896">46</a></sup> (release 9.1).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Genome synteny</h3><p>We performed synteny searches to compare the <i>L. chinense</i> genome structure with that of the grape and <i>Amborella</i> genomes using MCscan<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1914">47</a></sup>, requiring at least five gene pairs per syntenic block. The resulting dot plots were inspected to confirm the paleoploidy level of <i>L. chinense</i> in relation to the other genomes by counting the syntenic depth in each genomic region.</p><p><i>K</i><sub>s</sub> values for homologous gene pairs were calculated as described in Maere et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Maere, S. et al. Modeling gene and genome duplications in eukaryotes. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 102, 5454–5459 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1927">48</a></sup>. Fourfold synonymous third-codon transversion position values were calculated for syntenic segments from the concatenated alignments and constructed by dividing the number of transversions at all fourfold degenerate third-codon positions by the number of fourfold degenerate third-codon positions.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>Orthogroups were constructed with 14 other sequenced plants—6 eudicots (<i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i>, <i>Coffea canephora</i>, <i>Ipomoea nil</i> and <i>Fraxinus excelsior</i>); 6 monocots (<i>Brachypodium distachyon</i>, <i>Xerophyta viscosa</i>, <i>Asparagus officinalis</i>, <i>Musa acuminata</i>, <i>Ananas comosus</i> and <i>Oryza sativa</i>); 1 basal angiosperm (<i>Amborella trichopoda</i>); and 1 gymnosperm (<i>Gnetum montanum</i>)—and three other magnoliid transcriptome datasets, including two sequenced in this study (<i>Magnolia grandiflora</i> and <i>Michelia alba</i>) and one available in Ibarra-Laclette et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Ibarra-Laclette, E. et al. Deep sequencing of the Mexican avocado transcriptome, an ancient angiosperm with a high content of fatty acids. BMC Genom. 16, 599 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1989">49</a></sup> (<i>Persea americana</i>), using the software OrthoFinder<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Emms, D. M. &amp; Kelly, S. OrthoFinder: solving fundamental biases in whole genome comparisons dramatically improves orthogroup inference accuracy. Genome Biol. 16, 157 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d4932e1997">50</a></sup>. We selected low-copy orthogroups with the number of putative orthologues less than two in each species, and putative orthologues were found in at least four eudicots, four monocots, three magnoliids, one basal angiosperm and one gymnosperm, resulting in 1,163 orthogroups. Then, each orthogroup was aligned using Clustal Omega<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Sievers, F. &amp; Higgins, D. G. Clustal Omega for making accurate alignments of many protein sequences. Protein Sci. 27, 135–145 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2001">51</a></sup>, and all alignments were further trimmed using TrimAl 1.2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Capella-Gutierrez, S., Silla-Martinez, J. M. &amp; Gabaldon, T. TrimAl: a tool for automated alignment trimming in large-scale phylogenetic analyses. Bioinformatics 25, 1972–1973 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2005">52</a></sup>). Next, we constructed 1,163 single-gene trees using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2009">53</a></sup> with the PROTCATWAG mode. Then, we compared these single-gene trees with the species tree and screened them as described in Zeng et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Zeng, L. et al. Resolution of deep angiosperm phylogeny using conserved nuclear genes and estimates of early divergence times. Nat. Commun. 5, 4956 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2013">6</a></sup>. Finally, after careful examination, a total of 502 low-copy orthogroups were selected for further analysis.</p><p>We also calculated the phylogenetic signal based on three alternative topological hypotheses and quantified the difference in gene-wise log-likelihood scores (ΔGLS) among each of the three topologies using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Shen, X. X., Hittinger, C. T. &amp; Rokas, A. Contentious relationships in phylogenomic studies can be driven by a handful of genes. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 126 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2020">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2023">53</a></sup>. To diminish the influence of tiny amounts of data on phylogenetic inference, we further excluded orthogroups with outlier ΔGLS values, defined as described in Shen et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Shen, X. X., Hittinger, C. T. &amp; Rokas, A. Contentious relationships in phylogenomic studies can be driven by a handful of genes. Nat. Ecol. Evol. 1, 126 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2027">20</a></sup>. To estimate the species tree, we performed a coalescent-based approach using Astral 5.6.1 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Mirarab, S. et al. ASTRAL: genome-scale coalescent-based species tree estimation. Bioinformatics 30, i541–i548 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2031">54</a></sup>). We also performed phylogenetic analyses based on 78 chloroplast genes among 24 land plant species using RAxML<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Stamatakis, A. RAxML-VI-HPC: maximum likelihood-based phylogenetic analyses with thousands of taxa and mixed models. Bioinformatics 22, 2688–2690 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2035">53</a></sup>.</p><p>To estimate divergence time, we used PAML MCMCTREE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586–1591 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2042">55</a></sup> to perform Bayesian estimation with soft fossil constraints<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Yang, Z. &amp; Rannala, B. Bayesian estimation of species divergence times under a molecular clock using multiple fossil calibrations with soft bounds. Mol. Biol. Evol. 23, 212–226 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-018-0323-6#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d4932e2046">56</a></sup> based on 235 single-copy orthologous genes that are shared by <i>L. chinense</i> and 10 other species.
